The Baikal seal is the only freshwater species, though some ringed seals live in freshwater lakes in Russia close to the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic sea">Baltic sea</a>. In addition, harbor seals may visit <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>, lakes and rivers and sometimes stay as long as a year. Other species known to enter freshwater include California sea lions and South American sea lions.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Pinnipeds also use a number of terrestrial habitats and <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biology)" title="Substrate (biology)">substrates</a>, both continental and island. In temperate and tropical areas, they <a href="/wiki/Hauling-out" title="Hauling-out">haul-out</a> on to sandy and <a href="/wiki/Pebble" title="Pebble">pebble</a> beaches, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_shore" title="Rocky shore">rocky shores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">shoals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mud_flat" class="mw-redirect" title="Mud flat">mud flats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide_pool" title="Tide pool">tide pools</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Sea_cave" title="Sea cave">sea caves</a>. Some species also rest on man-made structures, like <a href="/wiki/Pier" title="Pier">piers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jetty" title="Jetty">jetties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buoy" title="Buoy">buoys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platforms</a>. Pinnipeds may move further inland and rest in sand dunes or vegetation, and may even climb cliffs.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Polar-living species haul-out on to both <a href="/wiki/Fast_ice" title="Fast ice">fast ice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">drift ice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior_and_life_history">Behavior and life history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Behavior and life history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phoca_vitulina_(Habor_Seal),_Point_Lobos,_CA,_US_-_Diliff.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg/220px-Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg/330px-Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg/440px-Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3722" data-file-height="2482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phoca_vitulina_(Habor_Seal),_Point_Lobos,_CA,_US_-_Diliff.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Harbor seal hauled out on rock</div></div></div>\n<p>Pinnipeds have an <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/amphibious" class="extiw" title="wikt:amphibious">amphibious</a> lifestyle; they spend most of their lives in the water, but haul-out to mate, raise young, molt, rest, <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulate</a> or escape from aquatic predators. Several species are known to <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migrate</a> vast distances, particularly in response to extreme environmental changes, like <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Ni\xc3\xb1o">El Ni\xc3\xb1o</a> or changes in ice cover. <a href="/wiki/Elephant_seal" title="Elephant seal">Elephant seals</a> stay at sea 8\xe2\x80\x9310&#160;months a year and migrate between breeding and molting sites. The northern elephant seal has one of the longest recorded migration distances for a mammal, at 18,000\xe2\x80\x9321,000&#160;km (11,000\xe2\x80\x9313,000&#160;mi). Phocids tend to migrate more than otariids.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Traveling seals may use various features of their environment to reach their destination including <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth&#39;s magnetic field">geomagnetic fields</a>, water and wind currents, the position of the sun and moon and the taste and temperature of the water.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pinnipeds may dive during foraging or to avoid predators. When foraging, Weddell seals typically dive for less than 15&#160;minutes to depths of around 400&#160;m (1,300&#160;ft) but can dive for as long as 73&#160;minutes and to depths of up to 600&#160;m (2,000&#160;ft). Northern elephant seals commonly dive 350\xe2\x80\x93650&#160;m (1,150\xe2\x80\x932,130&#160;ft) for as long as 20&#160;minutes. They can also dive 1,259\xe2\x80\x934,100&#160;m (4,131\xe2\x80\x9313,451&#160;ft) and for as long as 62&#160;minutes. The dives of otariids tend to be shorter and less deep. They typically last 5\xe2\x80\x937&#160;minutes with average depths to 30\xe2\x80\x9345&#160;m (98\xe2\x80\x93148&#160;ft). However, the New Zealand sea lion has been recorded diving to a maximum of 460&#160;m (1,510&#160;ft) and a duration of 12 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Walruses do not often dive very deep, as they feed in shallow water.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pinnipeds have lifespans averaging 25\xe2\x80\x9330&#160;years. Females usually live longer, as males tend to fight and often die before reaching maturity.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The longest recorded lifespans include 43&#160;years for a wild female ringed seal and 46&#160;years for a wild female grey seal.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The age at which a pinniped sexually matures can vary from 2\xe2\x80\x9312&#160;years depending on the species. Females typically mature earlier than males.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foraging_and_predation">Foraging and predation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Foraging and predation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg/280px-Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg" width="280" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg/420px-Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg/560px-Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1296" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steller sea lion with <a href="/wiki/White_sturgeon" title="White sturgeon">white sturgeon</a></div></div></div>\n<p>All pinnipeds are <a href="/wiki/Carnivorous" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnivorous">carnivorous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predatory" class="mw-redirect" title="Predatory">predatory</a>. As a whole, they mostly feed on fish and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bivalve" class="mw-redirect" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">endothermic</a> ("warm-blooded") prey like <a href="/wiki/Sea_bird" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea bird">sea birds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> While most species are <a href="/wiki/Generalist_and_specialist_species" title="Generalist and specialist species">generalist</a> and opportunistic feeders, a few are specialists. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Crabeater_seal" title="Crabeater seal">crabeater seal</a>, which primarily eats <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">ringed seal</a>, which eats mainly crustaceans, the <a href="/wiki/Ross_seal" title="Ross seal">Ross seal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_elephant_seal" title="Southern elephant seal">southern elephant seal</a>, which specialize on squid, and the <a href="/wiki/Bearded_seal" title="Bearded seal">bearded seal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a>, which feed on <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a> and other bottom-dwelling invertebrates.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Pinnipeds may hunt solitarily or cooperatively. The former behavior is typical when hunting non-schooling fish, slow-moving or immobile invertebrates or endothermic prey. Solitary foraging species usually exploit coastal waters, bays and rivers. An exception to this is the northern elephant seal, which feeds on fish at great depths in the open ocean. In addition, walruses feed solitarily but are often near other walruses in small or large groups that may surface and dive in unison. When large schools of fish or squid are available, pinnipeds such as certain otariids <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_hunting" title="Cooperative hunting">hunt cooperatively</a> in large groups, locating and herding their prey. Some species, such as California and South American sea lions, may forage with cetaceans and sea birds.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Seals typically consume their prey underwater where it is swallowed whole. Prey that is too large or awkward is taken to the surface to be torn apart.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The leopard seal, a prolific predator of penguins, is known to violently swing its prey back and forth until it is decapitated.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The elaborately <a href="/wiki/Cusp_(anatomy)" title="Cusp (anatomy)">cusped</a> teeth of <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">filter-feeding species</a>, such as crabeater seals, allow them to remove water before they swallow their planktonic food.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The walrus is unique in that it consumes its prey by <a href="/wiki/Suction_feeding" class="mw-redirect" title="Suction feeding">suction feeding</a>, using its tongue to suck the meat of a bivalve out of the shell.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta,_p.&#160;67_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta,_p._67-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> While pinnipeds mostly hunt in the water, South American sea lions are known to chase down penguins on land.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Some species may <a href="/wiki/Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith">swallow stones or pebbles</a> for reasons not understood.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Though they can drink seawater, pinnipeds get most of their fluid intake from the food they eat.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman31_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman31-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/220px-Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/330px-Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/440px-Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5305" data-file-height="3385" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fish8426_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">Leopard seal</a> capturing <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguin</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Pinnipeds themselves are subject to predation. Most species are preyed on by the <a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" title="Killer whale">killer whale</a> or orca. To subdue and kill seals, orcas continuously ram them with their heads, slap them with their tails and fling them in the air. They are typically hunted by groups of 10 or fewer whales, but they are occasionally hunted by larger groups or by lone individuals. Pups are more commonly taken by orcas, but adults can be targeted as well. Large <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> are another major predator of pinnipeds\xe2\x80\x94usually the <a href="/wiki/Great_white_shark" title="Great white shark">great white shark</a> but also the <a href="/wiki/Tiger_shark" title="Tiger shark">tiger shark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mako_shark" class="mw-redirect" title="Mako shark">mako shark</a>. Sharks usually attack by ambushing them from below. The prey usually escapes, and seals are often seen with shark-inflicted wounds. Otariids typically have injuries in the hindquarters, while phocids usually have injuries on the forequarters.<sup id="cite_ref-predation_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-predation-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Pinnipeds are also targeted by terrestrial and <a href="/wiki/Pagophilic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagophilic">pagophilic</a> predators. The <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bear</a> is well adapted for hunting Arctic seals and walruses, particularly pups. Bears are known to use sit-and-wait tactics as well as active stalking and pursuit of prey on ice or water. Other terrestrial predators include <a href="/wiki/Cougar" title="Cougar">cougars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_hyena" title="Brown hyena">brown hyenas</a> and various species of <a href="/wiki/Canid" class="mw-redirect" title="Canid">canids</a>, which mostly target the young.<sup id="cite_ref-predation_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-predation-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg/250px-Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg" width="250" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg/375px-Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg/500px-Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg 2x" data-file-width="962" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Killer_Whale_Tipe_B.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Killer whale hunting a Weddell seal</div></div></div>\n<p>Pinnipeds lessen the chance of predation by gathering in groups.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Some species are capable of inflicting damaging wounds on their attackers with their sharp canines\xe2\x80\x94an adult walrus is capable of killing polar bears.<sup id="cite_ref-predation_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-predation-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> When out at sea, northern elephant seals dive out of the reach of surface-hunting orcas and white sharks.<sup id="cite_ref-sleep_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sleep-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In the Antarctic, which lacks terrestrial predators, pinniped species spend more time on the ice than their Arctic counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Arctic seals use more breathing holes per individual, appear more restless when hauled out, and rarely defecate on the ice. Ringed seals build dens underneath fast ice for protection.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Interspecific predation among pinnipeds does occur. The leopard seal is known to prey on numerous other species, especially the crabeater seal. Leopard seals typically target crabeater pups, which form an important part of their diet from November to January. Older crabeater seals commonly bear scars from failed leopard seal attacks; a 1977 study found that 75% of a sample of 85 individual crabeaters had these scars.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Walruses, despite being specialized for feeding on bottom-dwelling invertebrates, occasionally prey on Arctic seals. They kill their prey with their long tusks and eat their blubber and skin. Steller sea lions have been recorded eating the pups of harbor seals, northern fur seals and California sea lions. New Zealand sea lions feed on pups of some fur seal species, and the South American sea lion may prey on <a href="/wiki/South_American_fur_seal" title="South American fur seal">South American fur seals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-predation_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-predation-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_behavior">Reproductive behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reproductive behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:16_Walross_2001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/16_Walross_2001.jpg/250px-16_Walross_2001.jpg" width="250" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/16_Walross_2001.jpg/375px-16_Walross_2001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/16_Walross_2001.jpg/500px-16_Walross_2001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="888" data-file-height="541" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:16_Walross_2001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walrus herd on ice flow</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mating_system" title="Mating system">mating system</a> of pinnipeds varies from extreme polygyny to <a href="/wiki/Serial_monogamy" class="mw-redirect" title="Serial monogamy">serial monogamy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Of the 33&#160;species, 20&#160;breed on land, and the remaining 13&#160;breed on ice.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Species that breed on land are usually polygynous, as females gather in large aggregations and males are able to mate with them as well as defend them from rivals. Polygynous species include elephant seals, grey seals and most otariids.<sup id="cite_ref-dimorphism_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dimorphism-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Land-breeding pinnipeds tend to mate on islands where there are fewer terrestrial predators. Few islands are favorable for breeding, and those that are tend to be crowded. Since the land they breed on is fixed, females return to the same sites for many years. The males arrive earlier in the season and wait for them. The males stay on land and try to mate with as many females as they can; some of them will even fast. If a male leaves the beach to feed, he will likely lose mating opportunities and his dominance.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Polygynous species also tend to be extremely sexual dimorphic in favor of males. This dimorphism manifests itself in larger chests and necks, longer canines and denser fur\xe2\x80\x94all traits that help males in fights for females. Increased body weight in males increases the length of time they can fast due to the ample energy reserves stored in the blubber.<sup id="cite_ref-dimorphism_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dimorphism-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Larger males also likely enjoy access to feeding grounds that smaller ones are unable to access due to their lower thermoregulatory ability and decreased energy stores.<sup id="cite_ref-GonzalezSuarezCassini2014_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GonzalezSuarezCassini2014-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In some instances, only the largest males are able to reach the furthest deepest foraging grounds where they enjoy maximum energetic yields that are unavailable to smaller males and females.<sup id="cite_ref-Le_Boefetal2000_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Le_Boefetal2000-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other seals, like the walrus and most phocids, breed on ice with copulation usually taking place in the water (a few land-breeding species also mate in water).<sup id="cite_ref-dimorphism_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dimorphism-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Riedman184_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman184-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Females of these species tend to aggregate less. In addition, since ice is less stable than solid land, breeding sites change location each year, and males are unable to predict where females will stay during the breeding season. Hence polygyny tends to be weaker in ice-breeding species. An exception to this is the walrus, where females form dense aggregations perhaps due to their patchy food sources. Pinnipeds that breed on fast ice tend to cluster together more than those that breed on drift ice.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman184_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman184-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Some of these species are serially monogamous, including the harp seal, crabeater seal and hooded seal.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman196_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman196-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Seals that breed on ice tend to have little or no sexual dimorphism. In lobodontine seals, females are slightly longer than males. Walruses and hooded seals are unique among ice-breeding species in that they have pronounced sexual dimorphism in favor of males.<sup id="cite_ref-dimorphism_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dimorphism-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Riedman184_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman184-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg/220px-Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg/330px-Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg/440px-Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2368" data-file-height="1581" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Callorhinus_ursinus_and_harem.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northern fur seal breeding colony</div></div></div>\n<p>Adult male pinnipeds have several strategies to ensure <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_success" title="Reproductive success">reproductive success</a>. Otariids establish <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territories</a> containing resources that attract females, such as shade, tide pools or access to water. Territorial boundaries are usually marked by natural breaks in the substrate,<sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> and some may be fully or partially underwater.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Males defend their territorial boundaries with threatening vocalizations and postures, but physical fights are usually avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-aggressive_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aggressive-117">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Individuals also return to the same territorial site each breeding season. In certain species, like the Steller sea lion and northern fur seal, a dominant male can maintain a territory for as long as 2\xe2\x80\x933 months. Females can usually move freely between territories and males are unable to coerce them, but in some species such as the northern fur seal, South American sea lion and Australian sea lion, males can successfully contain females in their territories and prevent them from leaving. In some phocid species, like the harbor seal, Weddell seal and bearded seal, the males have underwater territories called "maritories" near female haul-out areas.<sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> These are also maintained by vocalizations.<sup id="cite_ref-communication_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communication-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The maritories of Weddell seal males can overlap with female breathing holes in the ice.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman184_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman184-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg/220px-Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg/330px-Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg/440px-Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1938" data-file-height="2907" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_elephant_seal_combats.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male northern elephant seals fighting for dominance and females</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Lek_mating" title="Lek mating">Lek systems</a> are known to exist among some populations of walruses.<sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> These males cluster around females and try to attract them with elaborate <a href="/wiki/Courtship_display" title="Courtship display">courtship displays</a> and vocalizations.<sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Riedman212_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman212-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Lekking may also exist among California sea lions, South American fur seals, New Zealand sea lions and harbor seals.<sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In some species, including elephant seals and grey seals, males will try to lay claim to the desired females and defend them from rivals.<sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Elephant seal males establish <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)" title="Dominance (ethology)">dominance hierarchies</a> with the highest ranking males\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_(ethology)" title="Alpha (ethology)">alpha males</a>\xe2\x80\x94maintaining <a href="/wiki/Harem_(zoology)" title="Harem (zoology)">harems</a> of as many as 30\xe2\x80\x93100 females. These males commonly disrupt the copulations of their subordinates while they themselves can mount without inference. They will, however, break off mating to chase off a rival.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Grey seal males usually claim a location among a cluster of females whose members may change over time,<sup id="cite_ref-Lidgard_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lidgard-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> while males of some walrus populations try to monopolize access to female herds.<sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Male harp seals, crabeater seals and hooded seals follow and defend lactating females in their vicinity\xe2\x80\x94usually one or two at a time,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> and wait for them to reach estrus.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman196_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman196-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mating_114-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Younger or subdominant male pinnipeds may attempt to achieve reproductive success in other ways. Subadult elephant seals will sneak into female clusters and try to blend in by pulling in their noses. They also harass and attempt to mate with females that head out to the water. In otariid species like the South American and Australian sea lions, non-territorial subadults form "gangs" and cause chaos within the breeding rookeries to increase their chances of mating with females.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Alternative mating strategies also exist in young male grey seals, which do have some success.<sup id="cite_ref-Lidgard_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lidgard-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Female pinnipeds do appear to have some choice in mates, particularly in lek-breeding species like the walrus, but also in elephant seals where the males try to dominate all the females that they want to mate with.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman212_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman212-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> When a female elephant seal or grey seal is mounted by an unwanted male, she tries to squirm and get away, while croaking and slapping him with her tail. This commotion attracts other males to the scene, and the most dominant will end the copulation and attempt to mate with the female himself.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Dominant female elephant seals stay in the center of the colony where they are more likely to mate with a dominant male, while peripheral females are more likely to mate with subordinates.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Female Steller sea lions are known to solicit mating with their territorial males.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman212_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman212-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_parenting">Birth and parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Birth and parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With the exception of the walrus, which has five- to six-year-long inter-birth intervals, female pinnipeds enter <a href="/wiki/Estrous" class="mw-redirect" title="Estrous">estrous</a> shortly after they give birth.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> All species go through <a href="/wiki/Delayed_implantation" class="mw-redirect" title="Delayed implantation">delayed implantation</a>, wherein the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> remains in suspended development for weeks or months before it is implanted in the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a>. Delayed implantation postpones the birth of young until the female hauls-out on land or until conditions for birthing are favorable.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Riedman222_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman222-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">Gestation</a> in seals (including delayed implantation) typically lasts a year.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> For most species, birthing takes place in the spring and summer months.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Typically, single pups are born;<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> twins are uncommon and have high mortality rates.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman222_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman222-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Pups of most species are born <a href="/wiki/Precocial" class="mw-redirect" title="Precocial">precocial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg/220px-Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg/330px-Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg/440px-Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2480" data-file-height="1748" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harp_seal_mother_and_pup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Harp seal mother nursing pup</div></div></div>\n<p>Unlike terrestrial mammals, pinniped milk has little to no <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Mother pinnipeds have different strategies for maternal care and lactation. Phocids such as elephant seals, grey seals and hooded seals remain on land or ice and fast during their relatively short lactation period\xe2\x80\x93four days for the hooded seal and five weeks for elephant seals. The milk of these species consist of up to 60% <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>, allowing the young to grow fairly quickly. In particular, northern elephant seal pups gain 4&#160;kg (9&#160;lb) each day before they are weaned. Some pups may try to steal extra milk from other nursing mothers and gain weight more quickly than others. <a href="/wiki/Alloparenting" title="Alloparenting">Alloparenting</a> occurs in these fasting species;<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> while most northern elephant seal mothers nurse their own pups and reject nursings from alien pups, some do accept alien pups with their own.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg/220px-Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg/330px-Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg/440px-Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1182" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fur_seals_at_south_georgia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult Antarctic fur seal with pups</div></div></div>\n<p>For otariids and some phocids like the harbor seal, mothers fast and nurse their pups for a few days at a time. In between nursing bouts, the females leave their young onshore to forage at sea. These foraging trips may last anywhere between a day and two weeks, depending on the abundance of food and the distance of foraging sites. While their mothers are away, the pups will fast.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Lactation in otariids may last 6\xe2\x80\x9311 months; in the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_fur_seal" title="Gal\xc3\xa1pagos fur seal">Gal\xc3\xa1pagos fur seal</a> it can last as long as 3 years. Pups of these species are weaned at lower weights than their phocid counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Walruses are unique in that mothers nurse their young at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta76_128-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta76-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The female rests at the surface with its head held up, and the young suckle upside down.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Young pinnipeds typically learn to swim on their own and some species can even swim at birth. Other species may wait days or weeks before entering the water. Elephant seals do not swim until weeks after they are weaned.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Male pinnipeds generally play little role in raising the young.<sup id="cite_ref-Cassini_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cassini-136">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Male walruses may help inexperienced young as they learn to swim, and have even been recorded caring for orphans.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Male California sea lions have been observed to help shield swimming pups from predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Walker_2003_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_2003-138">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Males can also pose threats to the safety of pups. In terrestrially breeding species, pups may get crushed by fighting males.<sup id="cite_ref-Cassini_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cassini-136">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Subadult male South America sea lions sometimes abduct pups from their mothers and treat them like adult males treat females. This helps them gain experience in controlling females. Pups can get severely injured or killed during abductions.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Noaa-walrus12.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Noaa-walrus12.jpg/220px-Noaa-walrus12.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Noaa-walrus12.jpg/330px-Noaa-walrus12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Noaa-walrus12.jpg/440px-Noaa-walrus12.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="466" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Noaa-walrus12.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walrus males are known to use vocalizations to attract mates.</div></div></div>\n<p>Pinnipeds can produce a number of vocalizations such as barks, grunts, rasps, rattles, growls, creaks, warbles, trills, chirps, chugs, clicks and whistles. Vocals are produced both in air and underwater. Otariids are more vocal on land, while phocids are more vocal in water. Antarctic seals are more vocal on land or ice than Arctic seals due to a lack of terrestrial and pagophliic predators like the polar bear.<sup id="cite_ref-communication_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communication-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Male vocals are usually of lower frequencies than those of the females.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman335_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman335-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Vocalizations are particularly important during the breeding seasons. Dominant male elephant seals advertise their status and threaten rivals with "clap-threats" and loud drum-like calls<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman327_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman327-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> that may be <a href="/wiki/Vocal_resonation" title="Vocal resonation">modified</a> by the proboscis.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Male otariids have strong barks, growls, roars and "whickers". Male walruses are known to produce distinctive <a href="/wiki/Gong" title="Gong">gong</a>-like calls when attempting to attract females. They can also create somewhat musical sounds with their inflated throats.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman327_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman327-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Weddell seal has perhaps the most elaborate vocal repertoire with separate sounds for airborne and underwater contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-communication_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communication-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Underwater vocals include trills, chugs, chirps, chugs and knocks. The calls appear to contain prefixes and suffixes that serve to emphasize a message.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The underwater vocals of Weddell seals can last 70 seconds, which is long for a marine mammal call. Some calls have around seven rhythm patterns and are comparable to <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">birdsongs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whale_vocalization" title="Whale vocalization">whalesongs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Similar calls have been recorded in other lobodontine seals<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> and in bearded seals.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In some pinniped species, there appear to be geographic differences in vocalizations, known as <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> while certain species may even have individual variations in expression.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman328_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman328-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> These differences are likely important for mothers and pups who need to remain in contact on crowded beaches.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman335_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman335-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Otariid females and their young use <a href="/wiki/Contact_call" title="Contact call">mother-pup attraction calls</a> to help them reunite when the mother returns from foraging at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman335_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman335-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The calls are described are "loud" and "bawling".<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Female elephant seals make an unpulsed attraction call when responding to their young. When threatened by other adults or when pups try to suckle, females make a harsh, pulsed call.<sup id="cite_ref-Steward_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steward-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Pups may also vocalize when playing, in distress or when prodding their mothers to allow them to suckle.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steward_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steward-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> While most vocals are audible to the human ear, a captive leopard seal was recorded making <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasonic</a> calls underwater. In addition, the vocals of northern elephant seals may produce <a href="/wiki/Infrasonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Infrasonic">infrasonic</a> vibrations.<sup id="cite_ref-communication_118-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communication-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo,_Lancashire,_England_-female-8a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo%2C_Lancashire%2C_England_-female-8a.jpg/150px-Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo%2C_Lancashire%2C_England_-female-8a.jpg" width="150" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo%2C_Lancashire%2C_England_-female-8a.jpg/225px-Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo%2C_Lancashire%2C_England_-female-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo%2C_Lancashire%2C_England_-female-8a.jpg/300px-Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo%2C_Lancashire%2C_England_-female-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="955" data-file-height="1435" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zalophus_californianus_-Blackpool_Zoo,_Lancashire,_England_-female-8a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea lion balancing a ball</div></div></div>\n<p>Non-vocal communication is not as common in pinnipeds as in cetaceans. Nevertheless, when disturbed by intruders harbor seals and Baikal seals may slap their fore-flippers against their bodies as warnings. Teeth chattering, hisses and exhalations are also made as aggressive warnings. Visual displays also occur: Weddell seals will make an S-shaped posture when patrolling under the ice, and Ross seals will display the stripes on their chests and teeth when approached.<sup id="cite_ref-communication_118-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communication-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Male hooded seals use their inflatable nasal membranes to display to and attract females.<sup id="cite_ref-Berta73_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berta73-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligence">Intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Match-to-sample_task" title="Match-to-sample task">match-to-sample task</a> study, a single California sea lion was able to demonstrate an understanding of symmetry, transitivity and equivalence; a second seal was unable to complete the tasks.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> They demonstrate the ability to understand simple <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> and commands when taught an artificial <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign language</a>, though they only rarely used the signs <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantically</a> or logically.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, a captive California sea lion named Ronan was recorded bobbing its head in synchrony to musical rhythms. This "rhythmic entrainment" was previously seen only in humans, <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">parrots</a> and other birds possessing <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">vocal mimicry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In 1971, a captive harbor seal named <a href="/wiki/Hoover_(seal)" title="Hoover (seal)">Hoover</a> was trained to imitate human words, phrases and laughter.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> For sea lions used in entertainment, trainers toss a ball at the animal so it may accidentally balance it or hold the ball on its nose, thereby gaining an understanding of the behavior desired. It may require a year to train a sea lion to perform a trick for the public. Its long-term memory allows it to perform a trick after at least three months of non-performance.<sup id="cite_ref-Walker_2003_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_2003-138">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_relations">Human relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Human relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions">Cultural depictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cultural depictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pinnipeds_in_popular_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinnipeds in popular culture">Pinnipeds in popular culture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG/220px-1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG/330px-1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG/440px-1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3388" data-file-height="1788" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1883_Tabaksdose_in_Walrossgestalt_Yupik-Eskimo_anagoria.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walrus-shaped snuffbox. <a href="/wiki/Yupik" title="Yupik">Yupik</a>-<a href="/wiki/Eskimo" title="Eskimo">Eskimo</a>, 1883</div></div></div>\n<p>Various human cultures have for millennia depicted pinnipeds. The anthropologist, A. Asbj\xc3\xb8rn J\xc3\xb8n, has analysed beliefs of the <a href="/wiki/Celt" class="mw-redirect" title="Celt">Celts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a> who believed in <a href="/wiki/Selkie" title="Selkie">selkies</a>\xe2\x80\x94seals that could change into humans and walk on land.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Seals are also of great importance in the <a href="/wiki/Inuit_culture" title="Inuit culture">culture</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedmanintro_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedmanintro-156">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Inuit_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Inuit mythology">Inuit mythology</a>, the goddess <a href="/wiki/Sedna_(mythology)" title="Sedna (mythology)">Sedna</a> rules over the sea and marine animals. She is depicted as a <a href="/wiki/Mermaid" title="Mermaid">mermaid</a>, occasionally with a seal\'s lower body. In one legend, seals, <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a> and other marine mammals were formed from her severed fingers.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> One of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_coinage" title="Ancient Greek coinage">Ancient Greek coins</a> depicts the head of a seal, and the animals were mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>. The Greeks believed that seals loved both the sea and sun and were considered to be under the protection of the gods <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> people of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Peru" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Peru">ancient Peru</a> worshipped the sea and its animals, and often depicted sea lions in their art.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In modern <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>, pinnipeds are often depicted as playful and comical figures based on their performances in zoos, <a href="/wiki/Circus" title="Circus">circuses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal_park" title="Marine mammal park">marine mammal parks</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_captivity">In captivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: In captivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg/220px-Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg/330px-Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg/440px-Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2496" data-file-height="1664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kobe_oji_zoo_2009_April_22.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Captive sea lion at <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Oji_Zoo" title="Kobe Oji Zoo">Kobe Oji Zoo</a> Kobe, Japan</div></div></div>\n<p>Pinnipeds can be found in facilities around the world, as their large size and playfulness make them popular attractions.<sup id="cite_ref-zoo_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zoo-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Seals have been kept in captivity since at least <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a> and their trainability was noted by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Some modern exhibits have rocky backgrounds with artificial haul-out sites and a pool, while others have pens with small rocky, elevated shelters where the animals can dive into their pools. More elaborate exhibits contain deep pools that can be viewed underwater with rock-mimicking cement as haul-out areas. The most common pinniped species kept in captivity is the California sea lion as it is abundant and easy to train.<sup id="cite_ref-zoo_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zoo-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Other species popularly kept in captivity include the grey seal and harbor seal. Larger animals like walruses and Steller sea lions are much less common.<sup id="cite_ref-zoo_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zoo-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some organizations, such as the <a href="/wiki/Humane_Society_of_the_United_States" title="Humane Society of the United States">Humane Society of the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Animal_Protection" title="World Animal Protection">World Animal Protection</a>, object to keeping pinnipeds and other marine mammals in captivity. They state that the exhibits could not be large enough to house animals that have evolved to be migratory, and a pool could never replace the size and biodiversity of the ocean. They also state that the tricks performed for audiences are "exaggerated variations of their natural behaviors" and distract the people from the animal\'s unnatural environment.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>California sea lions are used in <a href="/wiki/Military_animal" title="Military animal">military applications</a> by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Navy_Marine_Mammal_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Navy Marine Mammal Program">U.S. Navy Marine Mammal Program</a>, including detecting <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">naval mines</a> and enemy divers. In the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, the animals have been trained to swim behind divers approaching a U.S. naval ship and attach a <a href="/wiki/Clamp_(tool)" title="Clamp (tool)">clamp</a> with a rope to the diver\'s leg. Navy officials say that the sea lions can do this in seconds, before the enemy realizes what happened.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Organizations like <a href="/wiki/PETA" class="mw-redirect" title="PETA">PETA</a> believe that such operations put the animals in danger.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The Navy insists that the sea lions are removed once their mission is complete.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting">Hunting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hunting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">Seal hunting</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Killing_fur_seals,_St_Paul_Island.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Killing_fur_seals%2C_St_Paul_Island.jpg/220px-Killing_fur_seals%2C_St_Paul_Island.jpg" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Killing_fur_seals%2C_St_Paul_Island.jpg/330px-Killing_fur_seals%2C_St_Paul_Island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Killing_fur_seals%2C_St_Paul_Island.jpg/440px-Killing_fur_seals%2C_St_Paul_Island.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2770" data-file-height="1591" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Killing_fur_seals,_St_Paul_Island.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Men killing northern fur seals on <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul_Island_(Alaska)" title="Saint Paul Island (Alaska)">Saint Paul Island</a>, Alaska in the mid 1890s</div></div></div>\n<p>Humans have hunted seals since the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>. Originally, seals were hit with clubs during haul-out. Eventually, seal hunters used <a href="/wiki/Harpoon" title="Harpoon">harpoons</a> to spear the animals from boats out at sea, and hooks for killing pups on ice or land. They were also trapped in nets. The use of firearms in seal hunting during the <a href="/wiki/Modern_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern era">modern era</a> drastically increased the number of killings. Pinnipeds are typically hunted for their meat and blubber. The skins of fur seals and phocids are made into <a href="/wiki/Fur_clothing" title="Fur clothing">coats</a>, and the tusks of walruses continue to be used for carvings or as ornaments.<sup id="cite_ref-hunting_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunting-166">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> There is a distinction between the subsistence hunting of seals by <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_peoples" title="Circumpolar peoples">indigenous peoples of the Arctic</a> and commercial hunting: subsistence hunters typically use seal products for themselves and depend on them for survival.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman111_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman111-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> National and international authorities have given special treatment to aboriginal hunters since their methods of killing are seen as less destructive and wasteful. This distinction is being questioned as indigenous people are using more modern weaponry and mechanized transport to hunt with, and are selling seal products in the marketplace. Some anthropologists argue that the term "subsistence" should also apply to these cash-based exchanges as long as they take place within local production and consumption. More than 100,000&#160;phocids (especially ringed seals) as well as around 10,000&#160;walruses are harvested annually by native hunters.<sup id="cite_ref-hunting_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunting-166">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FoA_protest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/FoA_protest.jpg/220px-FoA_protest.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/FoA_protest.jpg/330px-FoA_protest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/FoA_protest.jpg/440px-FoA_protest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2312" data-file-height="3015" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FoA_protest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protests of Canada\'s seal hunts</div></div></div>\n<p>Commercial sealing was historically just as important an industry as <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a>. Exploited species included harp seals, hooded seals, Caspian seals, elephant seals, walruses and all species of fur seal.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman111_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman111-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The scale of seal harvesting decreased substantially after the 1960s,<sup id="cite_ref-hunting_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunting-166">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> after the Canadian government reduced the length of the hunting season and implemented measures to protect adult females.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckman_2012_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckman_2012-168">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Several species that were commercially exploited have rebounded in numbers; for example, Antarctic fur seals may be as numerous as they were prior to harvesting. The northern elephant seal was hunted to near extinction in the late 19th century, with only a small population remaining on <a href="/wiki/Guadalupe_Island" title="Guadalupe Island">Guadalupe Island</a>. It has since recolonized much of its historic range, but has a <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottleneck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman111_167-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman111-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, the Mediterranean monk seal was extirpated from much of its former range, which stretched from the Mediterranean to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and northwest Africa, and only remains in the northeastern Mediterranean and some parts of northwest Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several species of pinniped continue to be harvested. The <a href="/wiki/Convention_for_the_Conservation_of_Antarctic_Seals" title="Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Seals">Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Seals</a> allows limited hunting of crabeater seals, leopard seals and Weddell seals. However, Weddell seal hunting is prohibited between September and February if the animal is over one year of age, to ensure breeding stocks are healthy. Other species protected are southern elephant seals, Ross seals and Antarctic fur seals.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman111_167-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman111-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The Government of Canada permits the hunting of harp seals. This has been met with controversy and debate. Proponents of seal hunts insist that the animals are killed humanely and the white-coated pups are not taken, while opponents argue that it is irresponsible to kill harp seals as they are already threatened by declining habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Caribbean monk seal has been killed and exploited by Europeans settlers and their descendants since 1494, starting with <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> himself. The seals were easy targets for organized sealers, fishermen, <a href="/wiki/Turtling_(hunting)" title="Turtling (hunting)">turtle hunters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buccaneer" title="Buccaneer">buccaneers</a> because they evolved with little pressure from terrestrial predators and were thus "<a href="/wiki/Island_tameness" title="Island tameness">genetically tame</a>". In the <a href="/wiki/Bahamas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a>, as many as 100 seals were slaughtered in one night. In the mid-nineteenth century, the species was thought to have gone extinct until a small colony was found near the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucat\xc3\xa1n Peninsula">Yucat\xc3\xa1n Peninsula</a> in 1886. Seal killings continued, and the last reliable report of the animal alive was in 1952. The IUCN declared it extinct in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese sea lion was common around the Japanese islands, but <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">overexploitation</a> and competition from fisheries drastically decreased the population in the 1930s. The last recorded individual was a juvenile in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As of 2013, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) recognizes 35&#160;pinniped species. With the Japanese sea lion and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_monk_seal" title="Caribbean monk seal">Caribbean monk seal</a> recently extinct, ten more are considered at risk, as they are ranked "<a href="/wiki/Critically_Endangered" class="mw-redirect" title="Critically Endangered">Critically Endangered</a>" (the Mediterranean and Hawaiian monk seals), "<a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">Endangered</a>" (Gal\xc3\xa1pagos fur seal, Australian sea lion, Caspian seal and Gal\xc3\xa1pagos sea lion), and "<a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a>" (northern fur seal, hooded seal and New Zealand sea lion). Three species\xe2\x80\x94the walrus, the ribbon seal, and the spotted seal\xe2\x80\x94have a "<a href="/wiki/Data_Deficient" class="mw-redirect" title="Data Deficient">Data Deficient</a>" ranking.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Species that live in polar habitats are vulnerable to the effects of recent and ongoing <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, particularly declines in sea ice.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> There has been some debate over the cause of the decline of Steller sea lions in Alaska since the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Depka.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Depka.jpg/220px-Depka.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Depka.jpg/330px-Depka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Depka.jpg/440px-Depka.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Depka.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Grey_seal" title="Grey seal">Grey seal</a> on beach occupied by humans near <a href="/wiki/Niechorze" title="Niechorze">Niechorze</a>, Poland. Pinnipeds and humans may compete for space and resources.</div></div></div>\n<p>Some species have become so numerous that they conflict with local people. In the United States, pinnipeds are protected under the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Mammal_Protection_Act_of_1972" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine Mammal Protection Act of 1972">Marine Mammal Protection Act of 1972</a> (MMPA). Since that year, California sea lion populations have risen to 250,000&#160;. These animals began exploiting more man-made environments, like docks, for haul-out sites. Many docks are not designed to withstand the weight of several resting sea lions. Wildlife managers have used various methods to control the animals, and some city officials have redesigned docks so they can better withstand use by sea lions.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Sea lions also conflict with fisherman since both depend on the same fish stocks.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, MMPA was amended to permit the lethal removal of sea lions from <a href="/wiki/Salmon_run" title="Salmon run">salmon runs</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bonneville_Dam" title="Bonneville Dam">Bonneville Dam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The 2007 law seeks to relieve pressure on the crashing <a href="/wiki/Oncorhynchus" title="Oncorhynchus">Pacific Northwest salmon</a> populations.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Wildlife officials have unsuccessfully attempted to ward off the sea lions using <a href="/wiki/Bomb" title="Bomb">bombs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubber_bullet" title="Rubber bullet">rubber bullets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bean_bag_round" title="Bean bag round">bean bags</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-warrant_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warrant-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Efforts to chase sea lions away from the area have also proven ineffective.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Critics like the Humane Society object to the killing of the sea lions, claiming that <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric dams</a> pose a greater threat to the salmon.<sup id="cite_ref-warrant_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warrant-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Similar conflicts have existed in South Africa with brown fur seals. In the 1980s and 1990s, South African politicians and fisherman demanded that the fur seals be <a href="/wiki/Culling" title="Culling">culled</a>, believing that the animals competed with commercial fisheries. Scientific studies found that culling fur seals would actually have a negative effect on the fishing industry, and the culling option was dropped in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pinnipeds are also threatened by humans indirectly. They are unintentionally caught in <a href="/wiki/Fishing_net" title="Fishing net">fishing nets</a> by <a href="/wiki/Commercial_fisheries" class="mw-redirect" title="Commercial fisheries">commercial fisheries</a> and accidentally swallow fishing hooks. <a href="/wiki/Gillnetting" title="Gillnetting">Gillnetting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">Seine netting</a> is a significant cause of mortality in seals and other marine mammals. Species commonly entangled include California sea lions, Hawaiian monk seals, northern fur seals and brown fur seals.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman111_167-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman111-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Pinnipeds are also affected by <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">marine pollution</a>. High levels of <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">organic chemicals</a> accumulate in these animals since they are near the top of <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a> and have large reserves of blubber. Lactating mothers can pass the toxins on to their young. These pollutants can cause <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_cancer" title="Gastrointestinal cancer">gastrointestinal cancers</a>, decreased reproductivity and greater vulnerability to infectious diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Other man-made threats include <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a> by oil and gas exploitation, encroachment by boats,<sup id="cite_ref-Riedman111_167-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman111-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> and underwater noise.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="/\xcb\x8c/: secondary stress follows">\xcb\x8c</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>; from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>pinna</i> "<a href="/wiki/Fin" title="Fin">fin</a>" and <i>pes, pedis</i> "foot".<sup id="cite_ref-Elias_2007_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elias_2007-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This term typically excludes the walrus in everyday English. In science, it is also sometimes restricted to the "true" seals of the family Phocidae.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Illiger, J.
